> off of warning regardless of how I do in future coursework.  My
> overall gpa is above a 2.0, but I guess that's being ignored.  I
> can't help but wonder if missing grade had anything to do with that.

If the IT person is in Admin stuff then you are right but not
everything is straight
Admin.  The dual major CS/EE or the Computer Engineering gets heavier
into Science
stuff.

There is a lot of math and applied math that is needed for CS.  When
you get a degree
in CS, you should be balanced such that you can do just about any type of app.
Assembly up to multi-language-multi-tier client server stuff.  You
might not be very
good at it but the degree says that you have been exposed to all of it.

If you are having trouble with physics and calculus, then maybe the CS
program and
the associated job market is not for you.


From johnxj at comcast.net  Wed Dec 19 19:49:52 2007
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jason Jordan)
Date: Wed, 19 Dec 2007 19:49:52 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Computer Science ap PSU a nightmare
In-Reply-To: <20071219183936.qkx491rrc0gssg0w@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <20071219183936.qkx491rrc0gssg0w@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <20071219194952.74577a92.johnxj@comcast.net>

On Wed, 19 Dec 2007 18:39:36 -0800
someone <plug_1 at robinson-west.com> dijo:

> Anyone else find PSU's computer science program next to impossible to get
> through?  If the physics and Calculus don't stop ya, it seems that Karla
> Fant will.  Programming Systems is called an intro course, but there's
> nothing intro about it.
> 
> I've run into difficulties with the academic policy.  Specifically,
> the academic warning policy.  PSU is in the dark ages when it comes
> to students dropping courses on time.  You go on warning if you
> cut your credit load in half.  PCC, last I checked, doesn't worry
> about credit load.  I dropped programming systems this fall, one
> of 2 courses I was signed up for.  I dropped it before it even
> started because the online format wasn't going to make the vague
> assignments any easier.  Write a pseudo real estate program is
> pretty out there for instructions.  Asking students to do this
> on their own in 2 weeks time seems downright absurd to me.
> 
> The advice from the IT world, I have a friend there, is to get a
> different degree and forget about computer science because I won't
> learn what I need to know anyways.  With 28 PSU credits so far and
> a few withdrawals, I'm worried about how long it will take to get
> off of warning regardless of how I do in future coursework.  My
> overall gpa is above a 2.0, but I guess that's being ignored.  I
> can't help but wonder if missing grade had anything to do with that.

I know nothing of the computer science program, but I have some of the
same issues in linguistics. At least I have managed a perfect 4.0 so
far, but only because I completely discarded the idea of getting a
degree from PSU. I am just taking what I want, so I'm free to take what
interests me and throw out the rest. Mind you, I have 2/3 of the
credits necessary for a masters degree from most any other institution,
but the degree requirements in linguistics at PSU make no sense for my
interests. It's all geared to people wanting to go abroad and teach
English to foreigners, which I have no interest in.

I have discussed this at some length with various professors and even
the department chair. Part of the problem is that PSU is under the
thumbs of the Oregon University System. So are the other universities
in the system -- OSU, UO, SO, etc. But the other universities were
there first, and they have greater clout at the state level, in spite
of the fact that PSU is bigger than any of them (which they resent). In
my case, UO offers the degrees that I want, so the Oregon University
System won't allow PSU to offer the same degree. It would be just
terrible if there was duplication, don't you see? 

Humans are by nature competitive. In the world of business competition
is all about making more profit. Universities aren't supposed to be
about profit, so the competition is toward prestige in other areas.
Just as in business, your prestige increases over the competition by
doing a better job, but it also increases over your competition by
killing them off. Thus the other universities in the system constantly
try to keep PSU from gaining more programs or expanding the ones they
have.

There are other issues as well. Universities must maintain
certification from <I forgot the name of the independent organization>.
The certification requirements are arcane, ridiculous and obsolete, but
PSU must still follow them. (E.g., you cannot maintain certification
unless you have a library with a minimum of 100,000 physical volumes.)
Without accreditation no other university would accept PSU credits for
transfers, and wouldn't even recognize degrees. In the case of the
Department of Applied Linguistics accreditation reqjuirements mean we
cannot offer a doctoral program until we have at least six full
professors. We have five, and OUS won't grant us permission to hire
another one. I state that not because it has anything to do with
computer science, but just to point out that there are literally
volumes of accreditation requirements that PSU has no control over.

I agree with your friend's suggestion that you consider just discarding
the degree and doing what you want. In the world of computer science
this might not limit employment opportunities as much as it would in
other fields. I'd also strongly consider your friend's suggestion to
get a different degree. I'd also suggest that you camp out in your
advisor's office until you get this stuff under control. Your advisor's
job is to keep this stuff from happening to you. Otherwise, all I can
do is commiserate.

And now to weigh in.  College is five goals (and more):

1) Learning how to think independently
2) Learning to put up with arbitrary nonsense
3) Networking
4) Vocational training
5) Learning to do the above without whining

If assignments are vague, then goal (1) is met.  If requirements
are stupid, then goal (2) is met.  Out here in The Real World (tm),
clients, suppliers, employers, and perhaps someday employees are
going to throw stuff at you that makes PSU look like structured,
rational perfection.  When you are out in T.R.W., there will be
two groups of people - the ones who have time to listen to whining,
and those who don't.  Which group would you guess is more profitable
to do business with?

If you want the diploma, you put up with the bullshit, because one
of the principal things a diploma signifies is an ability to absorb
punishment and get over hurdles.  Think of it as a tame, wussy sort
of boot camp, and be glad that it doesn't involve live fire and
crawling in bug-infested mud before dawn.

I have a great deal of respect for folks like John who treats college
as an intellectual adventure, and doesn't really care about the degree;
chances are, he will go on find new ways of looking at things, and
become a leader in his field (whatever that becomes).  If he is doing
this to get a day job that requires a degree, then he is crazy.
Credentials are needed for some things, knowledge is needed for some
things, and they are often different things.

Knowledge = Power
Time      = Money
Energy    = Power * Time
Time      = Energy / Power

therefore:

Money     = Energy / Knowledge

The job market rewards energy, not knowledge.  Sigh.

At the end of the day, the value of a college degree from a particular
institution is related to the respect that the graduates earn later on.
Somebody who trained in a coddled environment, and only knows how to
do the vocational stuff, isn't going to earn a lot of respect. 

Keith

Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

From ron at Opus1.COM  Thu Dec 20 00:35:47 2007
From: ron at Opus1.COM (Ronald Chmara)
Date: Thu, 20 Dec 2007 00:35:47 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] UPS Batteries
In-Reply-To: <47695073.7000106@frankhunt.com>
References: <47695073.7000106@frankhunt.com>
Message-ID: <C5480DBD-CEE7-470D-9156-54A92BD44241@opus1.com>


On Dec 19, 2007, at 9:10 AM, Frank Hunt wrote:
> Second question is what is the expected life span of those batteries?
> This set is only two years old.

Life Span: Depending on the reliability of your electrical system,  
and how loaded the battery is, 6 months to 36 months.

6 months: if you have frequent local black/brown-outs and surges (got  
AC? electrical heating on a thermostat? computers on the same circuit  
as a blow-dryer, clothes dryer, and home electric chair?), with the  
battery draining max power during events.

36 months: You live next to a power station, run nothing but  
computers in your house/computing center, and *every* computer you  
own has it's own 1500W UPS dedicated to the CPU (with optional UPS's  
for things like monitors, swtitches, etc.), and you never seem to  
suffer a major power outage.

So, YMMV. ;)

(mine sure does)

-Bop

someone <plug_1 at robinson-west.com> dijo:
Anyone else find PSU's computer science program next to impossible to
get through?  If the physics and Calculus don't stop ya, it seems
that Karla Fant will.  Programming Systems is called an intro course,
but there's nothing intro about it.
The discussion is not about Linux, so I am replying on plug-talk.
Perhaps one of the "arcane, useless" skills that future employers
may find essential is the ability to post to the proper email list.
The discussion or Portland State's computer science degree in general
is not about Linux?

A Linux specialist needs to know: bash, perl, python, and php more than
Java and C++.  C is important because the kernel is written in it.
This discussion is relevant from the standpoint of, is it worth it if
your interest lies in Linux to get a CS degree?  I'm leaning towards
no until the mandatory courses are a) passable without special help,
and b) relevant to a Linux environment.  I shouldn't have to ask a
special tutor that I have to compete with 200 people for how to
understand and approach my programming assignments.  Nor should I
have to put up with incompetent tutors, a problem I ran into with
physics at PCC.  College isn't the work world, you pay a lot of money
to access so called higher education where there's a service to be
rendered to you.  Yes, you have to take care of yourself.  It's an
academic setting, the professor isn't supposed to fous on confusing
people.

As an aside, I'd note that you aren't in much position to judge *what*
you might have missed, as the data you collected in that region are
rather sparse.  We'd know better if you had taken other computer
classes and found them also missable.

Carlos> Maybe it pays to find a CS department that specializes in the
Carlos> area you're interested in.  I would think that OSU would be
Carlos> much more linux-oriented, being a key datacenter in the linux
Carlos> universe.

I was exposed to programming in four classes as an undergraduate: 

 a) an intro to Mechanical Engineering class had a brief section on Fortran
 b) a later Computing in Engineering class taught Fortran all term
 c) an introduction to Computer Science class taught Pascal.
 d) a unix/c class taught C

Everything else, programming-wise I learned by reading and doing.
Later on, when I was taking some classes at OGI, I ran into masters
students in Computer Science that didn't seem to know much about
programming at all, which surprised me at first.  I am not saying they
were useless.  I expect they knew things I didn't.  But they were not
well skilled at turning ideas into good programs.

I think it is generally good advice to *do* the (exclude playing video
games here) thing you like doing, because that's how you get good at
it.  And being good at that thing makes you valuable to others who
might be willing to pay you.  And if it all works out, you end up
getting paid for doing something you like doing.


-- 
Russell Senior, Secretary
russell at personaltelco.net

I agree with most of what Keith said.

I finished my CS degree at PSU in 2005, and I must say, you didn't read the

From mattrae at gmail.com  Thu Dec 20 11:05:47 2007
From: mattrae at gmail.com (Matt Rae)

Message-ID: <789ddbf30712201105l42e99785pcbcfee62df10942@mail.gmail.com>

I struggled through getting a CS degree and I admit that many courses
I've taken I'll never use again. The only reason I took them was to
fulfill degree requirements. A degree is an excuse to make more money
and shows that you put your time in. If your interviewer has a degree
they expect that you got paddled with the same sadistic physics course
that they did. That gives you something in common. Its the same sort
of thing with fraternity hazing or military boot camp.

Its up to you what you get out of college. There are courses you'll
draw from daily. Its important is to be a life long learner and
college is a good place to soak up new ideas and interact with smart
people.

Still, almost everything you get out of college you could learn from
the internet or diving in and getting real world experience. The
barrier to learning and getting that experience is low.. (Example:
Google will even pay you to get experience developing open source
software).. With access to the internet there is a lifetime of
learning available on pretty much any topic. If you have the
motivation to learn on your own and do something important, that
outweighs going in to debt getting a degree.

Matt Rae

From ed at alcpress.com  Thu Dec 20 11:37:47 2007

> A Linux specialist needs to know: bash, perl, python, and php more than
> Java and C++.

This list seems arbitrary. Of these, bash is the only one that
might be considered mandatory, though a Linux specialist may
choose another shell. My servers run more Java code (code
that runs in a servlet container) than Perl and Python. You
won't find any PHP code on my systems but you will find Tcl,
Scheme, and Lua. I'm considering Forth for a future application.

I think it's useful to expose students to the universe of
possibilities instead of restricting them to the fashion trends.

Ed


From alan at clueserver.org  Thu Dec 20 13:12:13 2007
From: alan at clueserver.org (Alan)
Date: Thu, 20 Dec 2007 13:12:13 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Computer Science at PSU a nightmare
In-Reply-To: <476AC48B.70802@alcpress.com>
References: <20071220020342.dgcjsdsp8o8ccks8@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
	<476AC48B.70802@alcpress.com>
Message-ID: <37516.198.182.194.170.1198185133.squirrel@clueserver.org>

>> A Linux specialist needs to know: bash, perl, python, and php more than
>> Java and C++.
>
> This list seems arbitrary. Of these, bash is the only one that
> might be considered mandatory, though a Linux specialist may
> choose another shell. My servers run more Java code (code
> that runs in a servlet container) than Perl and Python. You
> won't find any PHP code on my systems but you will find Tcl,
> Scheme, and Lua. I'm considering Forth for a future application.
>
> I think it's useful to expose students to the universe of
> possibilities instead of restricting them to the fashion trends.

Enforcing fashion trends is what college is all about.

The needs of a "Linux Specialist" is different than those needed by a
Computer Science specialist.  (I will argue against specialization at this
point.  You should know all things well, not just Linux, not just
computers.  There is always something to learn. As Heinlein said,
"Specialization is for insects".)

The one language all Linux "experts" should know is "C".  (Not C++ or
Objective C, but C.)  That is what the kernel is written in.  It is what a
large number of applications are written in.  C++ is an easy step once you
understand C.  (And I said "understand", not "able to code in".)  If you
are using OS X, you should learn Objective C as well.

>From there, you need to know Perl and Python.  That is what a large chunk
of your tools are written in, if they are not in C.

Java is not as big of a player in Linux because the compilers have all had
closed source issues until recently.  This may change.  (Unless you are
doing web programming.  Then you need to know Java.)

If you are doing web programming you will also need to know PHP and
Javascript.  (I can hear Randal spinning in his grave at this point.)  You
may not like those languages, but if you program for the web, you will
need to know them at some point.  (Even if it is to know them enough to
know why you don't want to use them for any particular task.)

For shell programming, BASH is good to know.  From there you can pick up
the other variants.  If you are using BASH to any great degree, you should
learn awk and sed as well.  (It means you are probably working with legacy
code and the two seem to go together.)

If you are learning Computer Science, you have the above to some extent
(especially C), but there are others as well.

You should know scheme and lisp and haskel.  You should know at least two
architectures of assembly. You should know Bison.  You should be able to
understand the structure of languages, not just the command set.  You
should know not only how the programs fit together, but why.  (At this
point, new languages should not be an issue because they are just
variations on a theme.) You should understand the underlying structures of
data and how to construct them efficiently.

And if you really want to be an expert, you should know all of the above
and more.

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Thu Dec 20 17:33:22 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Thu, 20 Dec 2007 17:33:22 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] seek()able compressed files?
Message-ID: <200712201733.22582.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

Hi all,

I've been reading about Tim Bray's "Wide Finder" project, where he's 
basically looking at "grep for multicore" in the context of a 4-million 
line apache logfile (that's about a 1GB file without compression) on an 
8-core sun machine.

  http://www.tbray.org/ongoing/When/200x/2007/09/20/Wide-Finder

Having recently written a clusterable apache log processing tool, this 
piqued my interest, particularly the conclusion that the 8 cores can 
process the file faster than any disk can deliver it to the CPU.  So, 
the times on his page are based on a hot cache and require mmap'ing the 
entire file.  A hot disk cache is great if you're doing benchmarks, but 
not so great if you're in the real world where you're stuck doing 
something useful (e.g. processing new files all the time.)

Yeah, a raid1 of 3.0Gb/s sata disks would theoretically keep the 8 cores 
fed at 0.75GB/s -- just fast enough to keep up with the perl code, but 
I'm guessing that the mmap has to read the entire file before any work 
can start (or -- graciously assuming a lazy/parallel mmap() -- at least 
the rindex() call needs to be looking at the end of the 1GB string for 
the Nth process to get started.)

In my own experiments, I found that it is faster to read gzip'd data off 
of the disk/nfs and then process the other end of a gunzip pipe (kernel 
runs the pipe concurrently for free -- yay!)  Particularly when you're 
getting into clustering, the gigabit ethernet of the fileserver is 
still going to fill-up at some point, so slinging 1/12th of the data 
around and carrying a slight gunzip overhead on the node's CPU is a win 
(even given ethernet trunking at the server, etc.)

Ok, for those who are still with me...  How do I parallelize the gunzip 
or bunzip2 process for a single file?  That is, say I want 4 nodes to 
work on one file -- is it possible (or worth it) to attempt to seek() 
into the compressed file, re-sync the decompression stream, and then 
start working?  From my reading of the gzip and bzip format specs, it 
seems that you could conceivably find the start of the Nth compressed 
block, and start decompression -- but it doesn't appear that the 
compressed blocks are labeled in any convenient way (even when 
the --rsyncable option is given to gzip.)  Yes, both spec's state that 
the format is not intended to be randomly accessible.  My reading also 
implies that the gzip data may not even be byte-aligned, at which point 
I'm guessing the whole re-sync thing would be a no-go due to the 
overhead of all that bit-twiddling (not to mention the 8x increase to 
the feeling-around time.)

Does seek() over NFS even gain me anything?  That is, would the node 
still be pulling all of the data up to the seek point or does NFS 
bypass the intermediate data and only ship what gets read() down the 
wire?

Break the files into smaller chunks and use the filesystem as my seek()?  
That's what I've been concluding thus-far.

Alternatively, any thoughts on a chunkable compression format or aspects 
of alternate network filesystems that I'm overlooking?

--Eric
-- 
"Beware of bugs in the above code; I have only proved it correct, not
tried it."
--Donald Knuth
---------------------------------------------------
    http://scratchcomputing.com
---------------------------------------------------


From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Thu Dec 20 17:56:07 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Thu, 20 Dec 2007 17:56:07 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] seek() on NFS
In-Reply-To: <200712201733.22582.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <200712201733.22582.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
Message-ID: <200712201756.08046.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Eric Wilhelm
# on Thursday 20 December 2007 17:33:

>Does seek() over NFS even gain me anything?

Yes, indeed.  Gotta love NFS.

>That is, would the node 
>still be pulling all of the data up to the seek point or does NFS
>bypass the intermediate data and only ship what gets read() down the
>wire?

I just created a 1GB file (dd ... bs=1024k count=1024) and then appended 
a couple of lines to it -- seek($fh, 1024**3, 0) is practically 
instantaneous (0.009s) over a 100Mb/s link -- vs 90s if it does a 
read() through the whole file.

Similarly, mmap() to an offset seems to do the same thing as seek -- so 
perhaps the startup time of each process would be reduced vs the wf.pl 
(posted here:  http://www.cs.ucsd.edu/~sorourke/wf.pl) by simply 
calling mmap once from each process -- and for clusters, you can't just 
do the one mmap anyway because each job is potentially in a bank of ram 
on another machine.

--Eric
-- 
But you can never get 3n from n, ever, and if you think you can, please
email me the stock ticker of your company so I can short it.
--Joel Spolsky

    http://scratchcomputing.com

From mattrae at gmail.com  Thu Dec 20 18:16:19 2007
From: mattrae at gmail.com (Matt Rae)
Date: Thu, 20 Dec 2007 18:16:19 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] seek()able compressed files?
In-Reply-To: <200712201733.22582.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <200712201733.22582.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
Message-ID: <789ddbf30712201816mc856891s26345d7186157b4f@mail.gmail.com>

Interesting question. I took took a look just out of curiosity and
found this parallel gzip by maintainer of zlib:
http://zlib.net/pigz17.c.gz

Apparently the tool only parallelizes decompression. The bottom of the
header he says, "Inflation cannot be parallelized, at least not
without specially prepared deflate streams for that purpose." So
sounds like you'd have to use a specially compressed file if I
understand that right.

Matt Rae

> (even given ethernet trunking at the server, etc.)
>
> Ok, for those who are still with me...  How do I parallelize the gunzip
> or bunzip2 process for a single file?  That is, say I want 4 nodes to
> work on one file -- is it possible (or worth it) to attempt to seek()
> into the compressed file, re-sync the decompression stream, and then
> start working?  From my reading of the gzip and bzip format specs, it
> seems that you could conceivably find the start of the Nth compressed
> block, and start decompression -- but it doesn't appear that the
> compressed blocks are labeled in any convenient way (even when
> the --rsyncable option is given to gzip.)  Yes, both spec's state that
> the format is not intended to be randomly accessible.  My reading also
> implies that the gzip data may not even be byte-aligned, at which point
> I'm guessing the whole re-sync thing would be a no-go due to the
> overhead of all that bit-twiddling (not to mention the 8x increase to
> the feeling-around time.)
>
> Does seek() over NFS even gain me anything?  That is, would the node
> still be pulling all of the data up to the seek point or does NFS
> bypass the intermediate data and only ship what gets read() down the
> wire?
>
> Break the files into smaller chunks and use the filesystem as my seek()?
> That's what I've been concluding thus-far.
>
> Alternatively, any thoughts on a chunkable compression format or aspects
> of alternate network filesystems that I'm overlooking?
>
> --Eric
> --
> "Beware of bugs in the above code; I have only proved it correct, not
> tried it."
> --Donald Knuth

From iconoklastic at yahoo.com  Thu Dec 20 22:02:42 2007
From: iconoklastic at yahoo.com (Robert Kopp)
Date: Thu, 20 Dec 2007 22:02:42 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Sudden Samba problem
In-Reply-To: <476B4603.3000803@telestream.com>
Message-ID: <638763.76842.qm@web50604.mail.re2.yahoo.com>



m0gely <m0gely at telestream.com> wrote:So you have two Windows machines and one Linux machine connected to a 
LAN and suddenly network resources for printers and file sharing are 
unavailable on one windows machine to the other two machines. Is that 
correct?

So far the question is what changed on the one Windows machine?

It happened following a system scan with Norton on the Windows machine. The use of smbclient on the command line revealed this:

linux-vom8:/home/robert #  smbclient //christine/My_Pictures
Password:
Domain=[CHRISTINE] OS=[Windows 5.1] Server=[Windows 2000 LAN Manager]
tree connect failed: NT_STATUS_INSUFF_SERVER_RESOURCES
linux-vom8:/home/robert #    

This can be caused by insufficient IRPStackSize in the Windows registry. Remember, the Windows computer can still browse Linux shares, so the problem is not bidirectional. Anyhow, I'm going to try this--  

IRPStackSize is not explicitly stated in this case, so it defaults to 15. MS explains how to increase it:   

The IRPStackSize parameter specifies the number of stack locations in I/O request packets (IRPs) that are used by Windows 2000 Server, by Windows Server 2003, and by Windows XP. You may have to increase this number for certain transports, for media access control (MAC) drivers, or for file system drivers. Each stack uses 36 bytes of memory for each receive buffer. This value is set in the following registry subkey:
HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\LanmanServer\Parameters
The default value of the IRPStackSize parameter is 15. The range is from 11  (0xb hexadecimal) through 50 (0x32 hexadecimal).

If the subkey is not present, you can use Registry Editor to add the subkey. To do this, follow these steps:
1.    Click Start, click Run, type regedit, and then click OK.
2.    Locate and then click the following registry subkey:
HKEY_LOCAL_MACHINE\SYSTEM\CurrentControlSet\Services\LanmanServer\Parameters
3.    Click Edit, point to New, and then click DWORD Value.
4.    Type IRPStackSize, and then press ENTER to name the value.

Note Type IRPStackSize exactly as it is displayed. The value name is case sensitive.
5.    Click Edit, and then click Modify.
6.    In the Data Value box, type the value that is appropriate for the network, and then click OK.
If the problem persists after you complete this procedure, increase the value of IRPStackSize even more.




Robert "Tim" Kopp
http://analytic.tripod.com/

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Fri Dec 21 10:24:24 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Fri, 21 Dec 2007 10:24:24 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] mmap() is indeed lazy
In-Reply-To: <200712201733.22582.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
References: <200712201733.22582.ewilhelm@cpan.org>
Message-ID: <200712211024.25050.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Eric Wilhelm
# on Thursday 20 December 2007 17:33:

>I'm guessing that the mmap has to read the entire file before any work
>can start (or -- graciously assuming a lazy/parallel mmap() -- at
> least the rindex() call needs to be looking at the end of the 1GB
> string for the Nth process to get started.)

With some further testing, I've found that Sys::Mmap does indeed seek to 
the offset given to rindex() rather than reading the whole file first.  
So, finding something from the end of a 1GB string which has been 
mmap()ed starts just as quick as seek().

But, distributing the job across multiple nodes is still no fun when you 
can only fill them with a total 1/8 GB/s (the link from the fileserver 
is full.)

Perhaps that explains why Tim's t5120 runs wf.pl faster at 32 jobs than 
8 despite having only 8 cores?  Each core is bored waiting on input for 
a bit unless the incoming data is split more than 8 ways?  (Though I 
suspect that the 8-core 1.4GHz UltraSPARC T2 CPU is a bit different 
than a pile of x86 chips.)

  http://www.tbray.org/ongoing/When/200x/2007/10/30/WF-Results

Nope.  Dual core just acts like two cpus.  Linux has dealt with dual
processor systems for years.  Nothing has to be specially written for it,
apps-wise.  It just works like any multi-processor system.


From creswick at gmail.com  Fri Dec 21 12:42:20 2007
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Fri, 21 Dec 2007 12:42:20 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Are dual core processors really worth it?
In-Reply-To: <20071221121827.ts0hq21qock0gc44@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <20071221121827.ts0hq21qock0gc44@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec80712211242k1d457fdcg5513185caed368cd@mail.gmail.com>

On Dec 21, 2007 12:18 PM, someone <plug_1 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
> If software has to be written a special way to take advantage of dual core
> processors, do they offer any real benefit?

My understanding is that dual cores offer almost all the benefits of
multiple single-core processors.  (IIRC, running multiple processors
usually allows for higher memory bandwidth, since each proc has it's
own pipe & cache, but I'm no expert.)

Assuming that's the case, then any multitasking environment will
realize some benefits from dual cores.  One application can be running
on one core while another app is using the second core.  I've been
using SMP machines for years, and I've always found that a lower
clockspeed will result in similar performance when multitasking.

Typically, desktop applications have been written with one processor
in mind.  Most of the computation is done in a single thread, with
possible exceptions for IO-bound operations.  It's generally
non-trivial to take an existing single-threaded application and make
it multi-treaded, and allow each thread to run independently.  Issues
with shared memory and synchronization are very difficult to work out,
especially when you're trying to deal with an existing design and
implementation that probably doesn't have accurate documentation.  Add
to that the fact that it's usually extremely difficult to reproduce
bugs related to race conditions, and the whole process becomes quite a
nightmare.  Note that this is *only* of concern within each
application--as long as the OS and any run-time environments (such as
virtual machines) are capable of dealing with multiple cores /
multiple processors, then you will see some benefit to a dual core
processor.  There are also some algorithms for which there is no known
parallel approach--as far as we know the *must* be run sequentially.

There are pedantic cases where this isn't true, and of course if
you're only running one process that can't be parallelized, then you
won't see any benefit, but that's incredibly rare.

> Aside from 32 to 64 bit transition
> problems, do the newest processors and boards really make sense?  Look at
> Dell's Linux laptop, last I checked it runs Ubuntu in 32 bit mode on a
> dual core 64 bit machine.  Seems to me that dual core processors are a
> compatibility nightmare unless you are running the latest 64 bit OS.

I haven't looked at Dell's Linux offerings, but 32-bit OSes are still
better supported than 64-bit ones, and the 64-bit architecture is
backwards-compatible with 32-bit apps, so it's probably a lot easier
for them to just ship a 32-bit install.  We're still in that annoying
transitional period where the hardware is being pushed out without
great software support -- give it a few more years and 64-bit binaries
will be more easily found (and more easily built), and it will make
more sense to run a 64-bit OS.

--Rogan


From crownedgriffin at gmail.com  Fri Dec 21 12:46:41 2007

Subject: [PLUG] Are dual core processors really worth it?
In-Reply-To: <20071221121827.ts0hq21qock0gc44@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
References: <20071221121827.ts0hq21qock0gc44@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
Message-ID: <c6bc7d1e0712211246i645a20cet6e542738a841fe0d@mail.gmail.com>

I'm running XP x64 right now, and the only real compatibility issues I have
are with Microsoft's own products.  (I'm not using XP SP2 or greater
apparently..)

As for dual core, I just upgraded from a 2.0 GHz Athlon 64 to a
2.2GHzAthlon 64 X2 and the difference is amazing. Individual programs
themselves
don't really see a benefit. Running multiple programs at the same time is
where the real magic happens. No need to close Opera and Media Player any
more just because I want to play a game.

As far as 64bit vs. 32bit, there is really no difference. All the programs
out there are still 32bit.

On 12/21/07, someone <plug_1 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
>
> If software has to be written a special way to take advantage of dual core
> processors, do they offer any real benefit?  Aside from 32 to 64 bit
> transition
> problems, do the newest processors and boards really make sense?  Look at
> Dell's Linux laptop, last I checked it runs Ubuntu in 32 bit mode on a
> dual core 64 bit machine.  Seems to me that dual core processors are a
> compatibility nightmare unless you are running the latest 64 bit OS.
>
>      Michael Robinson

From ed at alcpress.com  Fri Dec 21 13:17:49 2007
From: ed at alcpress.com (Ed Sawicki)
Date: Fri, 21 Dec 2007 13:17:49 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Are dual core processors really worth it?
In-Reply-To: <c834ec80712211242k1d457fdcg5513185caed368cd@mail.gmail.com>
References: <20071221121827.ts0hq21qock0gc44@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
	<c834ec80712211242k1d457fdcg5513185caed368cd@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <476C2D7D.4050503@alcpress.com>

Rogan Creswick wrote:
> On Dec 21, 2007 12:18 PM, someone <plug_1 at robinson-west.com> wrote:
>> If software has to be written a special way to take advantage of dual core
>> processors, do they offer any real benefit?
> 
> My understanding is that dual cores offer almost all the benefits of
> multiple single-core processors.  (IIRC, running multiple processors
> usually allows for higher memory bandwidth, since each proc has it's
> own pipe & cache, but I'm no expert.)
> 
> Assuming that's the case, then any multitasking environment will
> realize some benefits from dual cores.  One application can be running
> on one core while another app is using the second core.  I've been
> using SMP machines for years, and I've always found that a lower
> clockspeed will result in similar performance when multitasking.
> 
> Typically, desktop applications have been written with one processor
> in mind.  Most of the computation is done in a single thread, with
> possible exceptions for IO-bound operations.  It's generally
> non-trivial to take an existing single-threaded application and make
> it multi-treaded, and allow each thread to run independently.  Issues
> with shared memory and synchronization are very difficult to work out,
> especially when you're trying to deal with an existing design and
> implementation that probably doesn't have accurate documentation.  Add
> to that the fact that it's usually extremely difficult to reproduce
> bugs related to race conditions, and the whole process becomes quite a
> nightmare.  Note that this is *only* of concern within each
> application--as long as the OS and any run-time environments (such as
> virtual machines) are capable of dealing with multiple cores /
> multiple processors, then you will see some benefit to a dual core
> processor.  There are also some algorithms for which there is no known
> parallel approach--as far as we know the *must* be run sequentially.
> 
> --Rogan

Taking advantage of multiple cores without having to deal with the
problems that Rogan describes above is one reason why I've been
refactoring some of my existing applications as numerous processes
running in a pipeline. Linux takes care of running the processes
on available cores/CPUs. I get the benefit without the headaches.

Many of my applications invoke processes like awk, sed, sort,
uniq, and other text processing tools. But just the other day
Alan said:

"If you are using BASH to any great degree, you should learn awk
and sed as well. It means you are probably working with legacy
code and the two seem to go together."

Geez, I don't want to run _legacy_ code, so I guess I should
stop doing this.  :-)

Ed


From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Fri Dec 21 13:24:12 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Fri, 21 Dec 2007 13:24:12 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Are dual core processors really worth it?
In-Reply-To: <c834ec80712211242k1d457fdcg5513185caed368cd@mail.gmail.com>
References: <20071221121827.ts0hq21qock0gc44@xerxes.robinson-west.com>
	<c834ec80712211242k1d457fdcg5513185caed368cd@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <200712211324.12509.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Rogan Creswick
# on Friday 21 December 2007 12:42:

>There are pedantic cases where this isn't true, and of course if
>you're only running one process that can't be parallelized, then you
>won't see any benefit, but that's incredibly rare.

Yes.  See my recent thread on the gunzip pipeline thing.  Even very 
simple stuff like `gunzip -c < foo.gz | wc -l` is running in 
parallel -- even on only a hyperthreaded processor it is faster (twice 
as fast) than going directly to the disk -- even on four WD 7200rpm 
sata disks in raid 10 (md mirror + lvm striping.)

My (aging) dual AMD 1.8Ghz MP 2200 workstation still puts the 
hyperthreaded 2.8Ghz P4 and (single) 3.2GHz xeon to shame on general 
productivity -- though the not-even-custom-built dual-core 64x2 3600 
that my wife is now running gives me workstation envy because my recent 
cluster job shows 58% more throughput on that machine than the dual MP 
(and that's just this silly little bookshelf acer practically-a-laptop 
micro atx thing with an onboard kitchen sink.)

Anybody built a 4x4 phenom machine yet?  My reading (and newegg) implies 
that it is still a unicorn, but it doesn't help that we've horribly 
garbled the jargon in the past year.  Now that you have more than one 
core in a socket -- is "4x4" 16 total cores or 4?  At one point, the 
forums seem to have gotten the notion that quad meant 2 cores or 
something silly like that.

--Eric
-- 
"But as to modern architecture, let us drop it and let us take
modernistic out and shoot it at sunrise."
--F.L. Wright

only common thing that still doesn't work is the Flash player in Firefox.

platforms yet. But just about everybody has working SMP, so both of the
cores are being exploited.

On Fri, 21 Dec 2007 18:08:03 -0800
John Jason Jordan <johnxj at comcast.net> dijo:

> Evidently it uses a different chip than the original, not the Atheros
> chip. 
> 
> lspci
> Ethernet controller: Marvell Technology Group Ltd. 88w8335 [Libertas]
> 80211b/g Wireless (rev 03)
> 
> I've spend an hour or so googling, but evidently the only solution is
> ndiswrapper. Does anyone know differently?

If you have 64-bit Linux, not even ndiswrapper can get it going. The problem is that there does not exist a 64-bit Windows driver, so ndiswrapper happily installs any of the umpteen 32-bit drivers that I tried, only to give me:

jjj at Devil7:~/Software/Ndiswrapper/V1.10$ dmesg |grep ndiswrapper
[  154.148879] ndiswrapper version 1.45 loaded (smp=yes)
[  154.282125] ndiswrapper (check_nt_hdr:153): kernel is 64-bit, but Windows driver is not 64-bit;bad magic: 010B
[  154.282131] ndiswrapper (load_sys_files:216): couldn't prepare driver 'wg511v2'
[  154.282204] ndiswrapper (load_wrap_driver:118): couldn't load driver wg511v2; check system log for messages from 'loadndisdriver'
[  154.285618] usbcore: registered new interface driver ndiswrapper

So this thing is junk. Luckily I paid only $17 for it.

Is anyone in PLUG-land successfully using a PCMCIA wireless card with 64-bit Linux?

wlan1 and the little green light is flashing.

(my internal Intel 4965AGN and the Netgear WG511). I suppose I could
just turn off the 4965AGN in the BIOS, since its the one that keeps
hanging up on me.

From johnxj at comcast.net  Sat Dec 22 18:50:58 2007

On Sat, 22 Dec 2007 17:57:55 -0800
Aaron Burt <aaron at bavariati.org> dijo:

> On Fri, Dec 21, 2007 at 06:08:03PM -0800, John Jason Jordan wrote:
> > Ethernet controller: Marvell Technology Group Ltd. 88w8335 [Libertas]
> > 80211b/g Wireless (rev 03)
> 
> Huh.  The "libertas" kernel module should support it.  But apparently
> not in your case.  (I'm kinda surprised that they used such a nice
> chipset.  Those are mostly used in embedded apps since they have all the
> smarts needed, and then some.)

Believe me, I googled for hours. In the case of the Netgear WG511 there
are three versions:

1) the original, using Atheros (the one Keith has several of - fully plug and play)
2) the second edition, using the Marvel Libertas chipset (mine)
3) the third edition, using a Prism chipset (problems, but I didn't follow up)

Netgear calls them all "WG511." Netgear thinks this is funny.

In all my googling I never found reference to a Linux "libertas" kernel
module, not even for the 32-bit users. I did discover, however, that
there are lots of different libertas devices. Furthermore, this chipset
appears in lots of different products, such as PCI wireless cards to use
in a desktop. It was from a German manufacturer of such a device that I
finally scored the 64-bit Windows driver. Neither Netgear nor Marvel
make 64-bit drivers, not even for Windows. Netgear probably thinks this
is funny too.

To add another related thought: In all my googling I was appalled at
the lack of an up-to-date site listing all known wireless devices and
details about using them with Linux. Everyone knows that wireless on
Linux can be a pain. Lack of information is a big part of that pain,
and there is no excuse for it.

Not really out of date, but definitely incomplete.  And hopefully not
hopelessly.  Compared to the state of affairs two years ago, I remain
hopeful.  The site reflects the current development effort to create a
coherent wifi stack in Linux and to provide open source drivers to
hook into that stack.  As I've said several times before on this list,
that work is incomplete, but it is getting better.

John> It doesn't even mention PCMCIA cards at all. And the sort is

The canonical response to criticism of that nature in the open source
world is:

 -ENOPATCH

Help make things better and they'll get better.  Whine about things
not being better and be prepared to watch them stay the same.  If you
can't help make things better, wait, and remember to say "Thank you"
when, eventually, they are.

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Sun Dec 23 15:06:23 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Sun, 23 Dec 2007 15:06:23 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] mismatched disk buffer with software raid 1 (mirror)
Message-ID: <200712231506.23462.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

Hi all,

I've got a couple of 160GB drives with 8MB buffer and now the 
otherwise-identical drives with the 16MB buffer are the sweet spot in 
the price curve.  (Specifically WD1600YS and WD1600AAJS, where the 
former has the bigger buffer and slightly lower seek time, but the 
latencies are supposedly the same.)

I'm considering using each of the old 8MB drives as the mirrors in 
software raid1 (plus striping via lvm), but I'm wondering if that will 
slow down the writes.

Any thoughts?  Does the buffer come into play during writes?

My hope is that the writes are just as fast (they have to be atomic, 
right?) and that the reads are only slower than they would be with 
4x16MB buffers in that the second pair doesn't pull quite as much 
weight.

This assumes that the raid driver distributes reads based on how much is 
left to be read (dispatching more work to each drive on-completion of 
the prior work unit) rather than just assuming that each spindle in a 
mirror will do half of the work and then waiting for both to answer.

Does a smaller drive buffer mean we all have to wait for the slow guy?

Thanks,
Eric
-- 
"Beware of bugs in the above code; I have only proved it correct, not
tried it."
--Donald Knuth

    http://scratchcomputing.com

On Sun, Dec 23, 2007 at 04:38:06PM -0800, Joe Shisei Niski wrote:
> Hi - anyone out there with experience getting their laptop to suspend or 
> hibernate under Ubuntu? i have two laptops i really love (Sager/Clevo, 
> nice high-end development boxes) but i haven't been able to get them to 
> suspend or hibernate unter Ubuntu 7.04 or 7.10, or with several 
> different versions of OpenSuse. Stanby & Hibernate work fine on Windows.

Hm.  How old are they, and have you updated the BIOS?  You might want to
do something like "dmesg | grep -i acpi" and see what it says.  I'm
gonna guess that the kernel doesn't like the version of ACPI on there.

I'll bet that it'll work OK if you add "acpi=force" to the kernel
commandline (either edit /boot/grub/menu.lst or try adding it
temporarily in GRUB at boot-time.)

Richard Stallman has been advising me on what to do about the whole Ultumix situation as far as the slander and the rejection I've been facing. So I sent him this message as a reply.
-----------------------------------------------------------------------------
Thank You For Your Knowledge and advice. I will call it Ultumix GNU/Linux. I thought that GNU meant the particular name of the free software packages such as PCLinuxOS's packages that make up their distro. 

I would love to work with someone giving them my ideas and letting them put them into practice but in the 5 years I've tried to get people to listen about making GNU/Linux more user friendly no one has listened to my ideas other than the users that don't know how to code (including myself) so thats why I'm trying my best to learn so I can take matters into my own hands.

I'm starting to think I should base it off of gentoo GNU/Linux instead. It seems their distro was designed for remastering. Of course I think It will take a month or so to do that. I'm going to cancel the 0.0.1.2 release for now because of the community that PCLinuxOS represents and the instability of their latest updates. 

Any help you have in the future would be much appreciated.

Sincerely yours in sharing the software

Justin Breithaupt.

Moonguide:/dev #

rsteff at Moonguide:~> x10 info
Interface clock: Tuesday, 21:02
Housecode = k

From creswick at gmail.com  Tue Dec 25 21:53:06 2007
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Tue, 25 Dec 2007 21:53:06 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Loss of symbolic link on re-boot
In-Reply-To: <4771E34B.1020307@comcast.net>

On Dec 25, 2007 9:14 PM, Richard C. Steffens <rsteff at comcast.net> wrote:
> Why would I lose a symbolic link when I re-boot the machine?
...
<snip>
...
> Moonguide:/dev # ls -l ttyX10
> /bin/ls: ttyX10: No such file or directory
> Moonguide:/dev # ln -s ttyS0 ttyX10
> Moonguide:/dev #
>
> rsteff at Moonguide:~> x10 info
> Interface clock: Tuesday, 21:02
> Housecode = k

/dev is probably not a "real" filesystem -- it's most likely created
by udev or devfs on each boot.  You'll need to dig around in the
configuration files for those systems to make an entry stick around.
Sorry I can't be of more help, but I don't know much about either udev
or devfs.

--Rogan

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Dec 26 06:15:06 2007
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 26 Dec 2007 06:15:06 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Loss of symbolic link on re-boot
In-Reply-To: <4771E34B.1020307@comcast.net>
References: <4771E34B.1020307@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0712260607330.3039@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Tue, 25 Dec 2007, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> Why would I lose a symbolic link when I re-boot the machine?

> I have a link to ttyS0 called ttyX10. ttyX10 disappears whenever I have to
> re-boot.

   As the two prior respondents wrote, the device goes away when you reboot
or unplug it. It's a result of the way udev works. Take a look at
/etc/udev/rules.d/udev.rules (at least that's where the file's located on my
Slackware box).

   I had the same issues when I replaced my serial-linking Kyocera smartphone
with the Treo USB-linking one. Learned a lot about udev in the process. When
I tailed /var/log/messages I'd see the device changing each time I plugged
the cable into the PDA. Two main lessons from this: 1) don't use softlinks
to the port; use the actual device name and 2) for my situation timing is
crucial; this doesn't apply to you.

   I'd set the cron jobs to reference /dev/ttyS0 (or /dev/tts/0 depending on
how your system is configured) and ignore the /dev/ttyX10 link.

HTH,

Rich

On Tue, 25 Dec 2007, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> I have a USB card reader that I use to download photos from the card from
> my camera. Most of the time it works. After the machine has been running
> for a number of weeks (I leave the machine on 24/7), if I plug in the card
> reader, the machine hangs.

   As more experienced folks on this list have written in response to my
USB-related issues, USB is complex and sometimes does "computer things" that
are immune to rational explanation. However, I have had a couple of
instances when my server/workstation would lock up totally: no pointer
movement via keys or trackball, keyboard totally unresponsive, could not
kill the X server.

   In both cases, I fired up my notebook and ssh'd into the box to kill X
remotely. That worked just fine. However, if you do this from another
machine on your network, take care to avoid the really stupid mistake I made
the first time: instead of exiting from the ssh connection before shutting
down the notebook, I forgot that I was remotely logged in and issued the
'halt' command. Oops! is too mild for what I said at the time.

   In a third case, as I was booting the notebook the three-finger-salute to
manually kill X worked after a long delay. There was some process that was
taking an inordinately long time to complete, and it blocked the keyboard
and trackball until done. I never did discover which process it was.

Rich

I find this one of the most annoying features of Linux:

1) that X is the most fragile component of Linux
2) that the out-of-band solution when X locks up is to ssh into the machine

I've found myself in plenty of situations where the kernel and all
other processes were running just fine but X hung and that it was
faster to turn off the machine than to connect another machine.  You'd
think there would be some keyboard mechanism to by-pass whatever
driver X is using to grab the keyboard so that you can get a virtual
terminal and reset X.

Or is there one and I'm unaware of it?

Regards,
- Robert

On Dec 26, 2007 7:13 AM, Robert Citek <robert.citek at gmail.com> wrote:

> You'd
> think there would be some keyboard mechanism to by-pass whatever
> driver X is using to grab the keyboard so that you can get a virtual
> terminal and reset X.
>
> Or is there one and I'm unaware of it?
>

Ctrl-Alt-Backspace may work.

If not, try Ctrl-Alt-F8 to start a new X session.  Use the new session to
Ctrl-Alt-Backspace and clean-start X entirely.  Alternatively, try
Ctrl-Alt-F1 to grab a console, root-login and 'telinit 3; telinit 5; exit'

Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Tue, 25 Dec 2007, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> 
>> Why would I lose a symbolic link when I re-boot the machine?
> 
>> I have a link to ttyS0 called ttyX10. ttyX10 disappears whenever I have to
>> re-boot.
> 
>    As the two prior respondents wrote, the device goes away when you reboot
> or unplug it. It's a result of the way udev works. Take a look at
> /etc/udev/rules.d/udev.rules (at least that's where the file's located on my
> Slackware box).

OK. I have 17 files in /etc/udev/rules.d/. The first one is
05-udev-early.rules. The last one is 95-udev-late.rules. I assume
someone came up with some reason why the various rules should be applied
in a certain order. (From man udev I understand that's how this works.)
So, if I want to put something in that won't be changed by something
else, should I put it in 95-udev-late.rules? Or should I create a new
.rules file, something like 97-my-own-udev.rules? I'll need to study
some more to understand just how to write the rule, but at least I found
the documentation.

>    I'd set the cron jobs to reference /dev/ttyS0 (or /dev/tts/0 depending on
> how your system is configured) and ignore the /dev/ttyX10 link.

It may be that the simplest thing to do would be to change the config
file for the X10 program and directly reference /dev/ttyS0, as you
suggest. That will mean re-installing the X10 program. A change to the
config file is only read during one of the iterations of make (I think).

Thanks, Rich, Rogan, and Eric, for the pointer to the solution.

Dick Steffens

-----Original Message-----
From: plug-bounces at lists.pdxlinux.org
[mailto:plug-bounces at lists.pdxlinux.org] On Behalf Of Richard C. Steffens
Sent: Wednesday, December 26, 2007 3:24 PM
To: General Linux/UNIX discussion and help; civil and on-topic
Subject: Re: [PLUG] Loss of symbolic link on re-boot

Rich Shepard wrote:
> On Tue, 25 Dec 2007, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> 
>> Why would I lose a symbolic link when I re-boot the machine?
> 
>> I have a link to ttyS0 called ttyX10. ttyX10 disappears whenever I have
to
>> re-boot.
> 
>    As the two prior respondents wrote, the device goes away when you
reboot
> or unplug it. It's a result of the way udev works. Take a look at
> /etc/udev/rules.d/udev.rules (at least that's where the file's located on
my
> Slackware box).

OK. I have 17 files in /etc/udev/rules.d/. The first one is
05-udev-early.rules. The last one is 95-udev-late.rules. I assume
someone came up with some reason why the various rules should be applied
in a certain order. (From man udev I understand that's how this works.)
So, if I want to put something in that won't be changed by something
else, should I put it in 95-udev-late.rules? Or should I create a new
.rules file, something like 97-my-own-udev.rules? I'll need to study
some more to understand just how to write the rule, but at least I found
the documentation.

>    I'd set the cron jobs to reference /dev/ttyS0 (or /dev/tts/0 depending
on
> how your system is configured) and ignore the /dev/ttyX10 link.

It may be that the simplest thing to do would be to change the config
file for the X10 program and directly reference /dev/ttyS0, as you
suggest. That will mean re-installing the X10 program. A change to the
config file is only read during one of the iterations of make (I think).

Thanks, Rich, Rogan, and Eric, for the pointer to the solution.

From ken at scottshill.com  Wed Dec 26 15:43:52 2007
From: ken at scottshill.com (Ken Hill)
Date: Wed, 26 Dec 2007 15:43:52 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Adding USB card reader hangs machine, sometimes
In-Reply-To: <4772E353.9030309@comcast.net>
References: <4771E32E.5060908@comcast.net> <4772E353.9030309@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <00e601c84819$2beeed40$83ccc7c0$@com>

Power. There are currently USB1 and USB2 types of ports/hardware. The USB2
port is much more power intensive compared to the USB1 port. If you are
plugging in a newer USB device into an older USB port, then the USB device
may want to expect a USB2 port; whereas the USB port may only support a USB1
external device. Make sense?

Ken

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Dec 26 15:57:16 2007
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 26 Dec 2007 15:57:16 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Loss of symbolic link on re-boot
In-Reply-To: <4772E280.70001@comcast.net>
References: <4771E34B.1020307@comcast.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0712260607330.3039@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<4772E280.70001@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0712261554510.3039@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Wed, 26 Dec 2007, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> So, if I want to put something in that won't be changed by something else,
> should I put it in 95-udev-late.rules? Or should I create a new
> .rules file, something like 97-my-own-udev.rules?

   Yes, you'd create your own file that is invoked after all the others. That
way as rule files change in distribution upgrades your file can remain
constant.

> It may be that the simplest thing to do would be to change the config file
> for the X10 program and directly reference /dev/ttyS0, as you suggest.

   Yup. It removes you from changes in udev and various devices
appearing/disappearing. Simple is better.

Rich

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Wed Dec 26 16:00:33 2007
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Wed, 26 Dec 2007 16:00:33 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Loss of symbolic link on re-boot
In-Reply-To: <00e501c84818$c5a2b990$50e82cb0$@com>
References: <4771E34B.1020307@comcast.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0712260607330.3039@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<4772E280.70001@comcast.net> <00e501c84818$c5a2b990$50e82cb0$@com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0712261557400.3039@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Wed, 26 Dec 2007, Ken Hill wrote:

> What is the benefit of using symbolic links?

   In this case there isn't a benefit. In other situations (e.g., a PDA,
CD-ROM) you can configure the hardware to mount using a generic symlink
(e.g., /dev/pilot, /dev/cdrom) and it doesn't matter where that link points.
You can change the actual device without the hardware knowing that's what
happened. It's an abstraction.

Rich

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Thu Dec 27 10:36:30 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Thu, 27 Dec 2007 10:36:30 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Loss of symbolic link in /dev on re-boot
In-Reply-To: <4773E0A5.7070405@comcast.net>
References: <4771E34B.1020307@comcast.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.64.0712261554510.3039@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<4773E0A5.7070405@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <200712271036.31232.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Richard C. Steffens
# on Thursday 27 December 2007 09:28:

>It occurs to me that another possibility would be to create the
> symbolic link in the same directory as the X10 program config file
> (so I'll know where to find it). That way, if sometime down the road
> I needed to change from ttyS0 to ttyS1 (or something like that), I
> could just recreate the link. And, the link would not go away on
> re-boot.

It just clicked for me that this is not a usb serial port -- guess I 
thought the regular ones were extinct :-D

Yes, it would be simpler to do without udev, but I think you might want 
to go with a line in rc.local ('ln -s ttyS0 /dev/X10') rather than 
having a link to a device outside of /dev (where one would otherwise 
expect a directory of regular files.)

Consider:

  grep foo /dev/ttyS0

That probably hangs because it never gets EOF -- at least that's how my 
treo behaves.  Thus, you'll be setting yourself up for a future 
head-scratching if you have a link to the device laying around 
somewhere and you do a `grep -r` there.

--Eric

From joeniski at easystreet.net  Thu Dec 27 11:46:37 2007
From: joeniski at easystreet.net (Joe Shisei Niski)
Date: Thu, 27 Dec 2007 11:46:37 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] laptop and suspend/hibernate woes
In-Reply-To: <20071224012040.GU4925@syrinxpc.com>

Thanks for the pointer, Aaron - i've been working on this for a few days.

One laptop is about six months old,  the other is just under 3 yrs old, 
and the latter's BIOS has been updated to no avail.

The symptoms of my suspend/hibernate problems are fairly common:
- suspend: screen goes blank, then the dialog appears showing that the 
screen is locked. When i unlock it, there's an alert telling me that 
there was a suspend problem and to check the docs.
- hibernate: screen flashes a bit, goes dark. Sometimes the "locked 
screen" dialog appears, sometimes not (depending on boot params) and i 
have to power-cycle the machine.

potentially useful lines from the 120 produced by "dmesg | grep -i acpi" 
on the newer one, with commentary in <>:
-------------
ACPI: BIOS bug: multiple APIC/MADT found, using 0
ACPI: If "acpi_apic_instance=2" works better, notify 
linux-acpi at vger.kernel.org
< using "acpi_apic_instance=2" on the kernel boot command line made no 
difference in behavior, although the first of these lines reflected that 
instance 2 was in use>

ACPI: Looking for DSDT in initramfs... error, file /DSDT.aml not found.
<this one disturbs me - i found a few references to it, but nothing that 
adresses it directly>

ACPI: System BIOS is requesting _OSI(Linux)
ACPI: Please test with "acpi_osi=!Linux"
<adding "acpi_osi=!Linux" to the command line made no difference, and 
these messages still appeared>

<the following lines tell me that hardware detection is working ok>
ACPI: Interpreter enabled
ACPI: (supports S0 S3 S4 S5)
ACPI: Using IOAPIC for interrupt routing

pnp: PnP ACPI init
ACPI: bus type pnp registered
pnp: PnP ACPI: found 12 devices
ACPI: ACPI bus type pnp unregistered
PCI: Using ACPI for IRQ routing

ACPI: CPU0 (power states: C1[C1] C2[C2] C3[C3])
ACPI: Processor [CPU0] (supports 8 throttling states)

ACPI: CPU1 (power states: C1[C1] C2[C2] C3[C3])
ACPI: Processor [CPU1] (supports 8 throttling states)
ACPI: Invalid passive threshold

 ACPI: Power Button (FF) [PWRF]
ACPI: Power Button (CM) [PWB]
ACPI: Sleep Button (CM) [SLPB]
ACPI: Lid Switch [LID]

< the last line repeats 9 or 10 times>
ACPI: Invalid passive threshold
------------------

i also tried boot parameters "pci=noacpi" which didn't fix 
suspend/hibernate but did significantly slow the loading of Gnome. 
"noapic" made no difference.

for what it's worth, i'm using the native Nvidia drivers, but have 
followed all the tips for modifying /etc/default/acpi-support and 
xorg.conf (which i know only too well from trying to get the other 
laptop with its ATI video to behave).

i've also tried disabling compiz (tried that first), though it made no 
difference in any of the command line boot options i ran through.

At one point, i saw tan error when trying to hibernate, "swsusp: cannot 
find swap device, try swapon -a" - it turns out that the failed 
hibernate attemps had corrupted the swap partition. Fixed that, and 
added "resume=UUID=<swap partition UUID>" to Grub, and it seems like 
swap works even after a hibernate failure.

i found this very detailed ACPI how-to on the Gentoo wiki, and may have 
to try the DSDT override, but i don't have the stomach for it right now.

http://gentoo-wiki.com/HOWTO_Fix_Common_ACPI_Problems

Again, thanks for the sugestion, it pointed me to more info than i'd 
previously found.


Joe

On Wed, 26 Dec 2007 11:25:47 -0800 (PST)
Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:

> On Wed, 26 Dec 2007, Aaron Burt wrote:
> 
> > You can switch to VCs from X with Ctrl-Alt-[F1-F6]
> 
> Aaron,
> 
>    If the keyboard is locked that's not an option.
> 
> Rich
> 

The magic sysrq key is designed to solve some of these problems.

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Magic_SysRq_key
or /(your-kernel-source)/Documentation/sysrq.txt

In particular the 'Alt-SysRq-r' key should switch the keyboard from raw
mode to XLATE.

You have to enable it in the kernel usually (I don't think it's the
default), with the CONFIG_MAGIC_SYSRQ option.

I haven't had this particular problem to test, but I have had occasion
to use the 

Alt-SysRq- s (for sync) - u (for remount read-only) - b (for instant
reboot)  sequence, which is a subset of the R-E-I-S-U-B sequence as
reference in the Wikipedia article.


-- 
Ross Brattain
ross at principia.edu

I don't know if you can do the same with that Linksys product, but the
Linksys NSLU2 runs Linux. You can do quite a bit with it. I don't own
one, but I've heard they are pretty nice.

http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/NSLU2
http://www.nslu2-linux.org/

Looks like you can get one for about $60 and hook it up to a big USB
hard drive for about $100. Total price ~$160 doesn't sound bad.

Matt

From joeniski at easystreet.net  Fri Dec 28 11:09:31 2007
From: joeniski at easystreet.net (Joe Shisei Niski)
Date: Fri, 28 Dec 2007 11:09:31 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Low cost fileserver
In-Reply-To: <789ddbf30712281055v5a39e707w4e0924cee6914c4b@mail.gmail.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0712171656220.4108@shadow>	<4774707F.3040400@telestream.com>
	<789ddbf30712281055v5a39e707w4e0924cee6914c4b@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <477549EB.5040106@easystreet.net>

i've been using an NSLU2 for over 3 years on our home net, it's 
outlasted one external hard drive. The throughput is what i'd expect, 
really quite adequate for backing up our PCs and acting as a 
general-purpose fileserver. Management features are limited, but 
adequate using the standard firmware - i looked at the open-source 
firmware, but didn't really feel a need for it.

The only real annoyance i've found is that it seems to be sensitive to 
the order in which you power up it and the attached disk(s).

Joe

From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Sat Dec 29 12:27:58 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)
Date: Sat, 29 Dec 2007 12:27:58 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Google calendar and konqueror
Message-ID: <200712291227.58977.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

Hi all,

These google apps are nifty, but they don't work in Konqueror.

Well, it seems that google is still following the 1995 practice of 
relying on a browser identifier instead of probing for features.

Go to settings->"configure konqueror"..."browser identification" and set 
a site-specific rule for telling google.com that you're "Safari 1.2.4 
on Mac OS X".

Sigh.  Lament.  Send complaints (I guess you have to check "I have 
another idea") to: 
http://www.google.com/support/calendar/bin/request.py?contact_type=feedback

--Eric

including the "for museum review" boxes. Those were sorted into "for
free tables" boxes and a couple of boxes that are sorted by the type
of machine. When i get a better sense of who's doing the museum stuff,
i'll run them by them.

That means there's just ONE MORE BOX to be gone through for
recycle/keep/free sorting. Then we need to figure out how to organize
the keepers, catalog and shelve them appropriately, label the shelves,
and figure out a checkout policy. We'll probably also want to figure
out an acquisition/wishlist policy if we want the library to be of use
instead of a random collection.
We'll probably also want better lighting and explanatory signage.

There's a double stack of books for the free table by the lamp, for
when the free table starts to look sparse.

There are also some brokenish looking desk/table units in the library
space that i trust will disappear in the course of the
everything-moving-around.

I'll be in next week. Seeya around!

"M. Edward (Ed) Borasky" <znmeb at cesmail.net> wrote: Robert Kopp wrote:
> With some effort, I installed Mondo Rescue on SuSE 10.3 x64. According to Mondo:

(Hey ... if it can insult you, I can insult it!) :)
I'd heard rave reviews for this software elsewhere--I believe it has been used by NASA (though it is no longer maintained), but apparently you have a dissenting opinion :-)

Anyhow, some people, including me, like to have an image backup of their system, such that one can boot from a CD and restore everything in the event that the installation becomes corrupted. Acronis TrueImage will do this, but the filesystem of the storage medium must be NTFS, and it uses a proprietary format for the backup. (Mondo uses .tar.) Anyhow, would you like to suggest something that serves this purpose better, preferably open-source?


Robert "Tim" Kopp
http://analytic.tripod.com/

There are oodles of rescue CDs that will do similar tasks. I'd go
hunting for the best documented, personally. A lot of people use Knoppix
that way, and it's certainly well documented, although it isn't nearly
as popular as it used to be. My systems are all Gentoo, and the Gentoo
LiveCD works just fine for everything I want to do.

There's a CD available with "CloneZilla" and "GParted" on it. The LiveCD
List (http://www.frozentech.com/content/livecd.php) has 35 "rescue" CDs
available. (But not Mondo) :)

By a strange quirk of fate, I buzzed through a number of them late last
week ... I'm looking for one that will boot without a monitor, so I can
bring a machine up headless.

On Mon, 31 Dec 2007, M. Edward (Ed) Borasky wrote:

> There are oodles of rescue CDs that will do similar tasks. ...

   There's a difference between a rescue CD and an image restore CD. If your
Gentoo hard drive crashed and you wanted to restore everything on a new
drive, exactly the way it was on the old drive, a rescue CD won't do the
job. Unless, that is, my understanding is flawed.

   I looked at Mondo several years ago, but decided that with my regular
backup tapes and the backup software on a USB flash drive I have all I need
to do an exact restore.

> system, such that one can boot from a CD and restore everything in the event
> that the installation becomes corrupted. Acronis TrueImage will do this, but
> the filesystem of the storage medium must be NTFS, and it uses a proprietary
> format for the backup. (Mondo uses .tar.) Anyhow, would you like to suggest
> something that serves this purpose better, preferably open-source?
>
>

> >> There are oodles of rescue CDs that will do similar tasks. I'd go

> >> week ... I'm looking for one that will boot without a monitor, so I can
> >> bring a machine up headless.

> > If you find one, would you please tell us about it?
> >
>
> I'm probably going to go for a hardware solution ;). But seriously, I
> didn't find any that looked like they'd work totally headless, but there
> are some that will work with a serial console. I think that's just a
> null modem cable from one machine to the other and some grub and
> /etc/inittab magic on the "headless" one.
>

Actually this type of stuff is in enterprise equipment already.  You
will have to
pay quite a bit more for that feature.  It is in the IPMI 2.0 and above.
Any server that supports IPMI Serial over LAN should be able to do this.
And since it is over LAN you should be able to do the install/restore from
anywhere in the world after someone locally installs the CD for you.  Better
yet on restore, network boot it from another device for no local person
requirement.


From scratchcomputing at gmail.com  Mon Dec 31 10:48:56 2007
From: scratchcomputing at gmail.com (Eric Wilhelm)

Subject: [PLUG] Headless machine rescue CD
In-Reply-To: <47791315.5060401@comcast.net>

	<47790DF9.3040707@cesmail.net> <47791315.5060401@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <200712311048.57067.ewilhelm@cpan.org>

# from Richard C. Steffens
# on Monday 31 December 2007 08:04:

>> By a strange quirk of fate, I buzzed through a number of them late
>> last week ... I'm looking for one that will boot without a monitor,
>> so I can bring a machine up headless.
>
>If you find one, would you please tell us about it?

The RIP - "Recovery is Possible" iso is pretty easy to customize.  I 
once created one that had a public key and IP address burned-in.  Maybe 
it even does DHCP out-of-the-box, but you probably want to at least 
stick a public key on it though (IIRC, ssh is disabled by default.)

There's also the memory stick angle.

Perhaps something that boots from a CD and reads site-specific details 
from a memory stick would be easiest to reconfigure without any iso 
remastering.  It's been a few years since I was tinkering with this 
though so perhaps that's already been done.

--Eric

From iconoklastic at yahoo.com  Mon Dec 31 13:16:43 2007
From: iconoklastic at yahoo.com (Robert Kopp)
Date: Mon, 31 Dec 2007 13:16:43 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Mondo configuration: Translation?
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.64.0712310926100.14665@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <435440.97438.qm@web50604.mail.re2.yahoo.com>



Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote: On Mon, 31 Dec 2007, M. Edward (Ed) Borasky wrote:

> There are oodles of rescue CDs that will do similar tasks. ...

   There's a difference between a rescue CD and an image restore CD. If your
Gentoo hard drive crashed and you wanted to restore everything on a new
drive, exactly the way it was on the old drive, a rescue CD won't do the
job. Unless, that is, my understanding is flawed.

   I looked at Mondo several years ago, but decided that with my regular
backup tapes and the backup software on a USB flash drive I have all I need
to do an exact restore.

G4L looks promising, though documentation is scanty. Those interested in it should probably join the mailing list.

A "recovery" solution is a removable and bootable OS that allows you to load an OS image back onto the disk that was imaged (or its replacement), and I would consider anything that does the job. All recovery CD's are live CD's, but not all live CD's are recovery CD's.


Robert "Tim" Kopp
http://analytic.tripod.com/
